            <div class="article-section__table-source"></div>
         </div>
         
         <p>To differentiate the origins of the three EPR components and their relationship to the Na ion storage process, temperature-dependent X-band CW EPR spectra were recorded across a temperature range from 295 to 5.4 K at selected potentials, as shown in <b>Figures</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202421976-fig-0003">3a</a>, <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information). Upon cooling the sample discharged to 0&nbsp;V, the EPR signal intensities of C1 and C2 show only a very slight increase, whereas the intensity of C3 increases significantly (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202421976-fig-0003">3b,c</a>; Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information). The slight intensity increase of C1 and C2 indicates electronic structures characteristic of a small band gap semiconductor or a metallic/semi-metallic species.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202421976-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202421976-bib-0024_R_d41648781e1036" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This observation supports the idea that C1 is attributed to the unpaired electron spins associated with sodiated HC, as the intercalation of cations between graphene layers modifies the electronic structure of HC, imparting paramagnetic species with metallic-like behavior to the material.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202421976-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202421976-bib-0025_R_d41648781e1041" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Adv. Phys. Phys. Rev. B">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For C2, its low intensity increase indicates that it originates from a sub-/nano-scale Na species with quasi-/metallic electronic properties, considering its narrow and sharp symmetric resonance line. In stark contrast, the intensity of C3 exhibits a significant temperature dependent behavior, increasing by a large factor (&gt;30) as the temperature is lowered from 295 to 5.4&nbsp;K, and the temperature dependence of C3 aligns broadly with the expected response for an <i>S&nbsp;</i> = &nbsp; electron spin governed by the Boltzmann distribution.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202421976-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202421976-bib-0026_R_d41648781e1049" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Such a response indicates that C3 arises from a localized radical that is relatively isolated and does not experience large electron-electron exchange interactions.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202421976-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/fb9b797b-b574-4660-be91-5b2eae2f99c5/adfm202421976-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/fb9b797b-b574-4660-be91-5b2eae2f99c5/adfm202421976-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/0b69c53d-d6a2-406c-aa43-06cc4a238943/adfm202421976-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/fb9b797b-b574-4660-be91-5b2eae2f99c5/adfm202421976-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202421976-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202421976" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) X-band CW EPR spectra of HC at a SOD of 0&nbsp;V, recorded across a temperature range from 295 to 5.4 K. b) Simulated EPR spectra at selected temperatures of 295 and 5.4&nbsp;K. c) Intensity verses temperature variation for C1, C2, and C3, derived from fits to the EPR spectra (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information). d) Room temperature X-band (9.8658&nbsp;GHz) and Q-band (34.00&nbsp;GHz) CW EPR spectra of HC at a SOD of 0&nbsp;V. e) Q-band (34.00&nbsp;GHz) CW EPR (295 K) spectrum and corresponding echo-detected field-swept spectra recorded at 295&nbsp;K (red) and 10&nbsp;K (blue). f) X-band (9.7835&nbsp;GHz) HYSCORE spectra of HC at a SOD of 0&nbsp;V. The insert shows the echo-detected field-swept spectrum and the horizontal line the experimental field position (348.8&nbsp;mT). In the weak coupling case observed here, signal to 1st order is centered around the Larmor frequency <i></i> (<sup>1</sup>H: 14.9&nbsp;MHz, <sup>13</sup>C: 3.7&nbsp;MHz) and split by the corresponding hyperfine interaction <i>A</i>, e.g. for <i></i>(<sup>1</sup>H)&nbsp; = &nbsp;14.9&nbsp;&nbsp;<i>A</i>/2&nbsp;MHz. g) Ex situ <sup>23</sup>Na MAS NMR spectra of the HC at different SODs. h) In situ synchrotron XRD patterns of the HC during the discharge process. The sharp peaks refer to the diffraction peaks of metallic Na. The X-ray wavelength used was 0.6877 . i) Schematic illustration of the transition of Na species from ionic to quasi-metallic to metallic states.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The interactions contributing to the EPR linewidths of three components were further investigated by a comparative study of the CW EPR spectra recorded at X-band (9.8658&nbsp;GHz) and Q-band (34.00&nbsp;GHz). The results in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202421976-fig-0003">3d</a> show that the linewidths of all three components remain essentially unchanged across both frequencies, indicating there is no appreciable broadening from anisotropy in the electron-Zeeman interactions (parameterized by <i>g</i>-values), which scale linearly with the magnetic field. For C1, this result supports the assignment of its broad width largely due to electron dipolar and exchange interactions, as well as lifetime broadening, which are consistent with an Na ion intercalation process. The characteristics of C2, which exhibits a homogeneously broadened line and averaged electron-Zeeman interactions due to itinerant electrons, further identifying it as being associated with quasi-/metallic Na species. C3, in contrast, describes localized paramagnetic centers associated with a carbon-based radical, which typically exhibit a small <i>g</i>-value anisotropy that is unresolved at Q-band frequencies due to the relatively broad spectrum width. This broadening is primarily governed by hyperfine interactions, which will be discussed in the following sections.</p>
         
         <p>To further study the properties of the three EPR components, pulse measurements were carried out. Echo-detected field-swept EPR measurements were performed by recording the echo intensity from a primary echo sequence (/2echo) as a function of the magnetic field <i>B</i><sub>0</sub>. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202421976-fig-0003">3e</a>, at the SOD of 0&nbsp;V, neither C1 nor C2 produce an electron spin echo, whereas an echo was observed for C3. For C1, the lack of an echo is likely due to rapid spin relaxation, where the phase memory time (<i>T</i><sub>M</sub>) of the echo is shorter than the spectrometer deadtime (100&nbsp;ns), preventing its detection. This interpretation aligns with the CW EPR data, where C1 remained unsaturated even under high microwave power irradiation at 5.4&nbsp;K (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information). The absence of an electron spin echo for C2 indicates a homogenously broadened line caused by the averaging of interactions by itinerant electrons. This observation is consistent with the weak signal intensity increase with lower temperature, and indicates that the unpaired electron spins of C2 are associated with small quasi-metallic or metallic species. Conversely, the observation of an echo for C3 confirms its linewidth is inhomogeneously broadened, implying that the radical is localized and that its spectral width is largely due to magnetic interactions with neighboring magnetic nuclei. Additionally, the electron spin echo for C3 was also detected at the SODs of 0.3 and 0.2&nbsp;V (Figures&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> and&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <p>C2 reveals the presence of quasi-/metallic Na species in HC during the discharge. However, due to their unique properties, these quasi-metallic Na species are inherently challenging to characterize.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202421976-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202421976-bib-0013_R_d41648781e1130" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Chem. Commun. Energy Environ. Sci.">13</a>, <a href="#adfm202421976-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202421976-bib-0027_R_d41648781e1130" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Energy Mater. J. Am. Chem. Soc. Natl. Sci. Rev.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To further investigate their formation, <sup>23</sup>Na MAS NMR characterizations were carried out, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202421976-fig-0003">3g</a>. In the NMR spectra, a broad, high-intensity peak is characteristic of diamagnetic ionic sodium, originating from overlapping signals due to intercalated Na ions, residual electrolyte salts, Na-containing species in the SEI, and surface adsorbed Na ions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202421976-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202421976-bib-0009_R_d41648781e1140" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Nano Lett. Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Energy Mater.">9</a>, <a href="#adfm202421976-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202421976-bib-0013_R_d41648781e1140" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Chem. Commun. Energy Environ. Sci.">13</a>, <a href="#adfm202421976-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202421976-bib-0027_R_d41648781e1140" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Adv. Energy Mater. J. Am. Chem. Soc. Natl. Sci. Rev.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The presence of this broad peak provides evidence that Na ions are intercalated within the HC structure in an ionic state. However, due to the inherently disordered nature of HC, precise characterization of these intercalated compounds is challenging, as significant signal overlap obscures the specific contributions from different sodium species. At the SOD of 0.1&nbsp;V, quasi-metallic Na clusters were detected, as evidenced by a broad peak corresponding to the <sup>23</sup>Na Knight shift between 500 and 700&nbsp;ppm. This suggests the reduction of Na<sup>+</sup> to Na<i><sup>n</sup></i><sup>+</sup> (0 &lt; <i>n</i> &lt;&nbsp;1) occurred. However, this peak shows a weak intensity due to the limited formation of quasi-metallic clusters at this stage. As the discharge progresses, this signal intensity increases and shifts to higher chemical shifts, reflecting an increased contribution from the Knight shift attributed to the enhanced Na 2s density of states at the Fermi level,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202421976-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202421976-bib-0013_R_d41648781e1157" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Chem. Commun. Energy Environ. Sci.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> with metallic Na being observed at a SOD of 0&nbsp;V.</p>
         
         <p>To understand the formation of metallic Na in HC, in situ synchrotron XRD analysis was carried out as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202421976-fig-0003">3h</a>. Using high-energy X-rays with a wavelength of 0.6877 , shorter than conventional lab XRD at 1.54184 , synchrotron XRD provides a higher resolution for detecting structural information of the sodium species formed in the HC. Metallic Na was observed at a very low voltage below 0.0148&nbsp;V. It is worth noting that this observation is crucial as it marks the first direct in situ detection of metallic Na formation in the HC anode during discharge. These results confirm the nature of C2 as a quasi-/metallic Na species. Therefore, there is an ionic to quasi-metallic to metallic transition of the Na species, as illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202421976-fig-0003">3i</a>. However, in the following charge process, it is found that the transition between ionic Na and metallic Na is only partially reversible, with some quasi-metallic Na persisting even after the full charge process (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>,&nbsp;Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <p>X-band hyperfine sublevel correlation spectroscopy (HYSCORE) was used to investigate electron-nuclear coupling to C3. The HYSCORE spectrum (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202421976-fig-0003">3f</a>) reveals that the paramagnetic center of C3 is coupled to nearby protons, with <sup>1</sup>H hyperfine couplings in the range |<i>A</i>(<sup>1</sup>H)|&nbsp;=&nbsp;228&nbsp;MHz. Additionally, there is an intense signal at the <sup>13</sup>C Larmor frequency with small hyperfine couplings, approximately |<i>A</i>(<sup>13</sup>C)|&nbsp; 1&nbsp;MHz, indicating C3 is also coupled to multiple carbon nuclei. These findings support the identification of C3 as a localized organic radical interacting with surrounding carbon atoms and protons, providing evidence for the occurrence of Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent co-intercalation in HC, given that the primary source of <sup>1</sup>H nuclei in the system would be the solvent molecules (diglyme, (CH<sub>3</sub>OCH<sub>2</sub>CH<sub>2</sub>)<sub>2</sub>O). It is feasible that C3 may result from electrolyte decomposition or the SEI. To investigate these possibilities, we studied the electrolyte decomposition and detected no EPR signals (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information). Additionally, in the charged HC (charged to 3&nbsp;V), only weak C3 signal was observed (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9a,b</a>, and Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). Furthermore, the C3 signal does not increase in intensity in the prolonged cycled HC, despite the increased SEI formation. This suggests that C3 is unlikely to be from the SEI, as its intensity would be expected to increase along with SEI growth. Thus, C3 is interpreted as being associate with Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent co-intercalation. It should be noted that the Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent co-intercalation has been extensively studied in graphite anodes but has rarely been reported in HC anodes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202421976-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202421976-bib-0028_R_d41648781e1220" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Energy Environ. Sci. Adv. Mater.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Previous studies have shown that HC exhibits structural differences depending on its synthesis temperature,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202421976-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202421976-bib-0029_R_d41648781e1227" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Chem. Mater. Chem. Mater.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which may potentially result in distinct Na ion storage mechanisms. Considering that the glassy carbon type HC used in the above experiments was synthesized at a high temperature above 2000 C, we also conducted EPR analysis on HC synthesized at a lower temperature of 1000 C, denoted as HC1000. The structural characterisations of HC1000 are detailed in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a> (Supporting Information), and the EPR analysis is presented in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a> (Supporting Information). At a SOD of 0&nbsp;V, the room temperature EPR spectrum of HC1000 shows two visible components: a component with a linewidth of 12&nbsp;mT, assigned to C1 (Na ion intercalation), and a second component with a linewidth of 0.55&nbsp;mT. Upon cooling from 296 to 12&nbsp;K, the EPR spectrum gradually converts into a single line with a width of 0.44&nbsp;mT at 12&nbsp;K. This behavior suggests that the narrow and broad EPR components observed at room temperature are electronically connected. As the temperature is lowered, increased conductivity enhances electron-electron exchange interactions between these components, leading to a single homogeneously broadened line. This effect makes it challenging to resolve a distinct metallic EPR signal at lower temperatures. Ex situ <sup>23</sup>Na MAS NMR characterization of the HC1000 reveals a quasi-metallic Na signal, supporting that Na ions undergo a transition to a quasi-metallic state. Although the results suggest the presence of C2 in HC1000, it remains difficult to precisely isolate this component in the EPR spectra, especially at low temperatures. Additionally, at low temperatures, a very weak electron spin echo was detected, allowing an EPR spectrum to be recorded that has position and linewidth characteristics of C3 in the above HC (glassy carbon type). This indicates a small population of isolated radicals, similar to what was observed in the HC (glassy carbon type), and is indicative of Na-solvent co-intercalation. However, the weak intensity of the signal prevented further analysis, such as obtaining proton coupling data from using HYSCORE. These findings collectively imply that Na ion intercalation, Na-solvent co-intercalation, and the transition from ionic to quasi-metallic Na states also occur in HC1000.</p>
         
         <p>To investigate the impact of Na ion intercalation on the electronic structure and surrounding binding environment in HC, as well as its role in the Na evolution mechanism from ionic to quasi-metallic and metallic states, density functional theory (DFT) analysis was conducted. Structural models are shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202421976-fig-0004">4a</a>; pristine graphene (pG) and Stone-Wales defect graphene (dG) layers with Na adsorbed on the surface (Na<sub>ads</sub>) and intercalated between the interlayer (Na<sub>inter</sub>), as well as combinations of adsorption and intercalation (Na<sub>ads</sub>-dG-Na<sub>inter</sub>). The DFT results in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202421976-fig-0004">4b</a> show that dG exhibits a more negative binding energy (7.921&nbsp;eV) compared to pG (7.696&nbsp;eV) when interacting with Na, indicating that the Stone-Wales defects enhance the surface sodiophilicity, making it more attractive for Na adsorption. The strong interactions lead to the formation of Na<sub>ads</sub>-C bonds with a bond length of 2.68&nbsp;, comparable to bond lengths observed in organosodium molecules (2.53.2&nbsp;). However, dG exhibits weaker interaction with intercalated Na (Na<sub>inter</sub>) between the interlayer, with a binding energy of 7.673&nbsp;eV, compared to 7.803&nbsp;eV for pG. Further calculations show that the pre-adsorption of Na<sub>ads</sub> on dG does not impact the binding of Na<sub>inter</sub> with the dG, as the binding energy maintains 7.671&nbsp;eV. This indicates that the initial adsorption of Na<sub>ads</sub> on the surface does not hinder the subsequent Na<sub>inter</sub> intercalation. However, intercalating Na<sub>inter</sub> significantly weakens the interaction between Na<sub>ads</sub> and dG, with the binding energy between the Na<sub>ads</sub>-dG decreasing to 5.985&nbsp;eV and the Na<sub>ads</sub>-C distance increasing to 9.12&nbsp;. This suggests that Na<sub>inter</sub> intercalation weakens the existing Na<sub>ads</sub>-dG interactions.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202421976-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/9a561207-321e-493b-8d03-43b9e1ab7364/adfm202421976-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/9a561207-321e-493b-8d03-43b9e1ab7364/adfm202421976-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/3b3216a7-f42b-4594-9f36-34218c8ce249/adfm202421976-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/9a561207-321e-493b-8d03-43b9e1ab7364/adfm202421976-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202421976-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202421976" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Projected structural models of the Na on (Na<sub>ads</sub>) and intercalated (Na<sub>inter</sub>) between planar pristine graphene (pG) and Stone-Wales defect graphene (dG) layers. b) Binding energy between Na and the graphene layers in different structural models. c) Isosurface of the charge density difference for Na<sub>ads</sub>-dG, dG-Na<sub>inter</sub> and Na<sub>ads</sub>-dG-Na<sub>inter</sub>. The yellow and cyan surfaces represent the gain and loss of charge density, respectively. Na is represented by purple spheres. d) PDOS of Na<sub>ads</sub>-dG, dG-Na<sub>inter</sub>, and Na<sub>ads</sub>-dG-Na<sub>inter</sub> models. e) Schematic illustration of the evolution of Na ions at the Stone-Wales defective sites.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>A charge density difference analysis reveals significant charge transfer between surface Na<sub>ads</sub> and dG, with the transferred charge delocalized around the Stone Wales defect sites (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202421976-fig-0004">4c</a>), facilitating the formation of Na<sub>ads</sub>-C interactions. When Na is intercalated between the interlayers, there is substantial charge transfer from Na<sub>inter</sub> to dG, which reduces the surface electron density of dG (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202421976-fig-0004">4c</a>, indicated by cyan), rendering the dG surface more positively charged. This increase in positive charge enhances Coulomb repulsion interaction between Na<sub>ads</sub> and dG, significantly weakening Na<sub>ads</sub>-C interactions. As these interactions rely on electron density donation from Na<sub>ads</sub> to the graphene matrix, their weakening implies that less valence electron density is transferred from Na<sub>ads</sub> to graphene. This allows Na<sub>ads</sub> to retain more of its valence electron density, driving it toward a quasi-metallic state.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202421976-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202421976-bib-0011_R_d41648781e1356" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Funct. Mater. Adv. Energy Mater. Adv. Mater. Nat. Energy">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The density of states (DOS) spectra of Na<sub>ads</sub> on the surface of Na<sub>ads</sub>-dG, shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202421976-fig-0004">4d</a>, feature broadened partial density of states (PDOS) of the Na <i>s</i> and <i>p</i> orbitals, indicating hybridization of Na orbitals with C orbitals promoted by electron transfer from the electropositive Na<sub>ads</sub> to defect sites. However, with the Na intercalation as illustrated in the Na<sub>ads</sub>-dG-Na<sub>inter</sub> model, sharp PDOS of the Na<sub>ads</sub> <i>s</i> and <i>p</i> orbitals are observed, with the total DOS significantly shifted to the right (a higher <i>EE</i><sub>f</sub>), confirming that less charge is transferred between Na<sub>ads</sub> and dG.</p>
         
         <p>Therefore, the Na ion intercalation between the dG layers drives the transition of the Na<sub>ads</sub> from ionic to quasi-/metallic as schematically illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202421976-fig-0004">4e</a>. In the sloping region, the Na ion intercalation does not occur intensively, which is insufficient to induce substantial charge transfer with the dG, resulting in only a small portion of ionic Na transitioning to a quasi-metallic state. This observation aligns with the detection of a very weak C2 signal in this voltage region. Upon further discharge into the plateau region, intensive charge transfer occurs between the intercalated Na<sub>inter</sub> and the dG, leading to much weaker Na<sub>ads</sub>-C interactions and facilitating the formation of quasi-metallic Na clusters. Ultimately, the continued aggregation and growth of these quasi-metallic Na clusters culminate in the formation of metallic Na, confirmed by the in situ synchrotron XRD characterization.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202421976-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202421976-sec-0030-title">3 Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The above characterizations reveal that the Na ion storage in HC involves multiple processes, including Na ion intercalation, Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent co-intercalation, and a transition of sodium species from ionic to quasi-/metallic. The EPR analysis of component C1 provides direct evidence of the Na ion intercalation process in HC, a detail that is challenging through XRD and in situ Raman data (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202421976-fig-0001">1e,f</a>). The Na ion intercalation process is illustrated in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202421976-fig-0005">5</a>. Initiated in the sloping region (stage 1), the Na ion intercalation process becomes more efficient in the subsequent plateau region, particularly in the voltage range between 0.1 and 0.01&nbsp;V (stage 2). This is implied by a significant increase in the signal intensity of C1, indicating an increasing concentration of intercalated Na ions in the HC.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202421976-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/8297f012-3ff8-4b5f-ada3-aaa5363dd6ce/adfm202421976-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/8297f012-3ff8-4b5f-ada3-aaa5363dd6ce/adfm202421976-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/87c52216-3500-49a3-9102-c433eeb8c80b/adfm202421976-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/8297f012-3ff8-4b5f-ada3-aaa5363dd6ce/adfm202421976-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202421976-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202421976" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Schematic illustration of the Na ion storage mechanism in the HC.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The EPR signal of C2, associated with sub-/nano-scale Na species exhibiting quasi-/metallic electronic properties, is observed throughout the reaction process. In situ synchrotron XRD and <sup>23</sup>Na MAS NMR characterizations further reveal that metallic Na is detected exclusively within the voltage range below 0.0148&nbsp;V (stage 3), whereas quasi-metallic Na species are identified above this voltage range (stage 1 and 2). These findings indicate that the type of Na species stored in the HC varies with the SOD. This sequential transition of Na speciesfrom ionic to quasi-metallic to metallicduring discharge provides crucial new insights into the dynamic electrochemical mechanisms in HC.</p>
         
         <p>Additionally, the detection of C3 reveals that Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent co-intercalation occurs in HC, a process that is difficult to detect using traditional characterization methods. From previous work, the co-interaction of Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent complexes has been identified as the primary mechanism that enables graphite to be an electrochemically active electrode for storing Na ions in ether-based electrolytes. However, this process typically occurs in the high voltage range.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202421976-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202421976-bib-0028_R_d41648781e1453" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed. Energy Environ. Sci. Adv. Mater.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, in the sloping region, the Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent co-intercalation is expected to be present in the short range HC domains that exhibit graphite-like order (stage 1), where the intercalation between the solvent molecules and the graphene layer helps to stabilize the distribution of Na ions in these regions. However, this process is limited at this stage, as C3 exhibits a very weak signal intensity, indicating that only a small percentage of Na ions are stored through this mechanism. The co-intercalation process becomes more prominent in the low voltage region below 0.01&nbsp;V (stage 3). Notably, C3 was also observed in the HC discharged in an ester-based electrolyte (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information). Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent co-intercalation is thus most likely to occur in specific areas that provide a niche environment with weak interlayer interactions, which are unable to fully desolvate the Na ions from their solvated state. These regions are likely to be certain pores between the carbon domains that are accessible to the electrolyte rather than the closed pores in the HC.</p>
         
         <p>Based on the above analysis, it can be seen that the microstructural properties of the HC play a crucial role in determining Na ion storage pathways. Optimizing these properties is essential for enhancing Na ion storage performance, which can be effectively accomplished through a carefully designed thermal treatment process. Furthermore, introducing Stone-Wales defects offers an additional strategy for increasing Na ion storage capacity. These defects promote surface Na ion adsorption and facilitate Na ion intercalation, providing an avenue to boost HC capacity through defect engineering. However, the progressive metallic transition of Na ions at defect sites, particularly the formation of metallic Na, poses a potential safety concern. Thus, precise control over defect concentration is critical to balance the enhanced Na ion storage capacity with mitigating metallic Na formation. Furthermore, Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent co-intercalation emerges as a promising mechanism for further increasing Na ion storage in HC.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202421976-sec-0040">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202421976-sec-0040-title">4 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>High-resolution CW and pulse EPR spectroscopy, combined with in situ experimental and theoretical investigations have enabled an in depth understanding of Na ion storage processes in the HC anode at an electronic level. Three distinct electrochemical processes are identified, including: Na ion intercalation between the graphene layers in both sloping and plateau regions, with a predominant contribution in the plateau region. In addition to this, Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent co-intercalation occurs in carbon domains with graphite-like order or in certain pores between the carbon domains, with the occurrence depending on the state of discharge. Furthermore, Na ions are adsorbed on Stone-Wales defect sites on the surface of graphene layers in the sloping region. These Na ions simultaneously evolve into quasi-metallic Na, which progressively undergo metallization within the plateau region, ultimately transitioning to metallic Na in the low voltage region below 0.01&nbsp;V. This transformation is driven by charge redistribution between intercalated Na ions and graphene layers, facilitated by continuous Na ion intercalation. This work demonstrates the unique capabilities of EPR spectroscopy in probing the charge storage mechanisms of battery electrode materials.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202421976-sec-0050">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202421976-sec-0050-title">5 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202421976-sec-0060">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202421976-sec-0070">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202421976-sec-0080">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202421976-sec-0080-title"> Sample Preparation</h4>
                  
                  <p>The hard carbon (HC) studied in this work was obtained from Thermo Fisher Scientific.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202421976-sec-0090">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202421976-sec-0090-title"> Structural Characterizations</h4>
                  
                  <p>The microstructure of the HC was characterized using a scanning transmission electron microscope (TEM, Hitachi HF5000), X-ray diffraction (XRD) with Cu K radiation source (Bruker D8) with an X-ray wavelength is 1.54184 , and Raman spectroscopy with a 514&nbsp;nm incident laser (Renishaw).</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202421976-sec-0100">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202421976-sec-0100-title"> Electrochemical Characterizations</h4>
                  
                  <p>To prepare the HC electrode, HC powder was mixed with polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF) in a weight ratio of 95:5 in N-Methyl-2-pyrrolidone (NMP) solution to form a uniform slurry. Then, the slurry was uniformly coated onto Cu foil and dried at 120 C for 24&nbsp;h in a vacuum oven. The as-prepared electrode was cut into pieces with a diameter of 10&nbsp;mm. The electrochemical performance was evaluated in three-electrode cells with the HC electrode as the working electrode; Na metal as the counter electrode and reference electrode, a glass fiber separator, and 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> in diglyme solvent as the electrolyte. The cells were assembled in a glove box under an Ar atmosphere with O<sub>2</sub> &lt;&nbsp;0.1&nbsp;ppm and H<sub>2</sub>O &lt;&nbsp;0.1&nbsp;ppm. Galvanostatic charge/discharge tests were conducted on a Biologic VMP-3 electrochemical workstation between 0 and 3.0&nbsp;V at a current density of 10&nbsp;mA g<sup>1</sup> at room temperature.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202421976-sec-0110">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202421976-sec-0110-title"> Sample Preparation for Ex Situ EPR Characterizations</h4>
                  
                  <p>To prepare the samples for ex situ EPR characterization, the cells were initially discharged to various SOD, specifically 0.5, 0.3, 0.2, 0.1, 0.01, 0&nbsp;V. Subsequently, the cells were disassembled in a glovebox, the resulting GC electrodes were washed with diglyme solvent and then dried under vacuum in a vacuum transfer chamber for 12&nbsp;h. Finally, the sodiated HC electrodes were sealed in Ar-filled thin-wall quartz tubes (3 mm outside diameter). For the temperature-dependent continuous-wave (CW) EPR characterizations, the quartz tubes were evacuated to a pressure of 1&nbsp;&nbsp;10<sup>4</sup>&nbsp;Pa, and then the tubes were sealed using a blow torch.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202421976-sec-0120">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202421976-sec-0120-title"> X-Band and Q-Band CW and Pulse EPR Spectroscopy Characterizations</h4>
                  
                  <p>X-band (ca. 9.8658&nbsp;GHz) CW EPR spectra were recorded on a Bruker Elexsys E500 spectrometer equipped with a Bruker Dual Mode resonator and He cooling (Bruker waveguide Cryogen-free system with recirculator, WVGD SYS 5K F70H wRCRC 2). The magnetic field was calibrated with 2,2-diphenyl-1-picrylhydrazyl (<i>g</i> = 2.0036), and measurements were carried out in the temperature range of 5295K.</p>
                  
                  <p>Q-band and all pulse data were acquired on a Bruker ElexE580 spectrometer equipped with a 1&nbsp;kW TWT Amplifier at X-band (Model 117X, Applied Systems Engineering, Inc) and a 150&nbsp;W TWT Amplifier at Q-band (Model 187Ka, Applied Systems Engineering, Inc), and a cryogen-free variable temperature cryostat (No. 3679A, Cryogenics, Inc). Q-band measurement used a Bruker ER5106QT-2 probehead (allowing 3&nbsp;mm tube access).</p>
                  
                  <p>The free induction decay (FID) detected field-swept spectra were acquired by integration of the signal (FID) from a single microwave pulse of length 500&nbsp;ns. The echo-detected field-swept spectra were acquired using the sequency of /2----echo, with <i>t</i><sub>/2</sub> = 100&nbsp;ns,  = 480&nbsp;ns, <i>t</i><sub></sub> = 200&nbsp;ns. For both techniques, the derivative of the recorded absorption spectrum was calculated numerically with MatLab using a 2nd order polynomial fitted 5 points either side of the intensity point being processed.</p>
                  
                  <p>X-band Hyperfine sublevel correlation (HYSCORE) experiments were carried out at 15&nbsp;K employing the pulse sequence /2  <i></i>  /2  <i>t</i><sub>1</sub>    <i>t</i><sub>2</sub>  /2  <i></i>&nbsp;-echo, using mw pulses of lengths <i>t</i><sub>/2</sub>&nbsp; = &nbsp;16&nbsp;ns, starting times <i>t</i><sub>1</sub>&nbsp; = &nbsp;<i>t</i><sub>2</sub>&nbsp; = &nbsp;32&nbsp;ns, <i></i>&nbsp; = &nbsp;134&nbsp;ns (matrix <sup>1</sup>H blind-spot), repetition time 1&nbsp;ms, and time increments of <i>t</i>&nbsp; = &nbsp;16&nbsp;ns (data matrix 160&nbsp;&nbsp;160).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202421976-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202421976-bib-0026_R_d41648781e1589" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26">26</a>, <a href="#adfm202421976-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202421976-bib-0030_R_d41648781e1589" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Chem. Phys. Lett.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> A four-step phase cycle was used to remove unwanted echoes. The observer position was the echo maximum at the center of the spectrum. The HYSCORE data were processed with MATLAB; time traces were baseline corrected with an exponential, apodized with a Gaussian window, and zero filled. After a 2D Fourier transformation absolute-value spectra were calculated.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202421976-sec-0130">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202421976-sec-0130-title"> Ex Situ <sup>23</sup>Na MAS NMR Characterization</h4>
                  
                  <p>Ex situ <sup>23</sup>Na MAS NMR characterization was conducted on a Bruker Avance III 300&nbsp;MHz spectrometer. First, the HC electrodes were discharged to the target SODs at a current density of 10&nbsp;mA g<sup>1</sup> in three-electrode cells. Then, the electrodes were collected, washed with DMC, and dried in a vacuum chamber attached to the glovebox. Before characterization, the HC was scraped from the Cu foil and sealed into zirconia rotors in a glovebox.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202421976-sec-0140">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202421976-sec-0140-title"> In Situ Raman Characterization</h4>
                  
                  <p>In situ Raman characterization was conducted with a Raman spectroscope (Renishaw) with a 514&nbsp;nm incident laser. A customized (MTI Corporation) in situ setup with a hole on the cathode side was used for the experiment. To prepare the HC electrode for the in situ Raman characterization, the HC was mixed with NMP in a weight ratio of 9:1 to form a uniform slurry. The slurry was coated onto a titanium mesh and dried at 120 C for 12&nbsp;h in a vacuum oven. The in situ cell was assembled using the as-prepared electrodes as the cathode, sodium metal as the anode, and glass fiber soaked with 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> in diglyme solvent as the electrolyte. The hole was sealed using a quartz window. The cell was discharged using a Biologic VMP-3 electrochemical workstation at a current density of 10&nbsp;mA g<sup>1</sup>. Raman spectra were collected in the spectra range 10002000&nbsp;cm<sup>1</sup>. A 50 objective was used to focus the laser light onto the electrode surface to collect the Raman signal. The laser power was set to 5&nbsp;mW, and the spectrum acquisition was finished in 30 s.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202421976-sec-0150">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202421976-sec-0150-title"> In Situ Synchrotron X-ray Diffraction Characterization</h4>
                  
                  <p>In situ synchrotron X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns were measured at the Powder Diffraction Beamline, Australian Synchrotron. An X-ray wavelength of 0.6877  was employed for the in situ characterization. The in situ cells were assembled using the HC as a working electrode, glass fiber separator, and sodium metal as a counter electrode in a commercial 2032-type coin cell with a hole punched through the casing (both sides) to allow X-rays to transmit. The holes were covered by the Kapton tape (practically X-ray transparent) to avoid exposing the electrode to air. The cells were galvanostatic discharged/charged during the tests at a current density of 10&nbsp;mA g<sup>1</sup> in a Neware battery test system.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202421976-sec-0160">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202421976-sec-0160-title"> Density Functional Theory Calculations</h4>
                  
                  <div class="paragraph-element">The calculations were performed within the framework of density functional theory (DFT) using the Vienna <i>Ab-initio</i> Simulation Package (VASP) code, employing the projector augmented wave method with the PerdewBurkeErnzerhof (PBE) exchange-correlation functional.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202421976-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202421976-bib-0031_R_d41648781e1649" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Phys. Rev. B Phys. Rev. B Phys. Rev. Lett.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Two structural models of pristine graphene and Stone-Wale defect-containing graphene were considered. In the models, there are 64 C and 2 Na<sub>ads</sub> in the models Na<sub>ads</sub>-pG, Na<sub>ads</sub>-dG, and Na<sub>ads</sub>-dG-Na<sub>inter</sub>, with 2 Na<sub>inter</sub> in Na<sub>ads</sub>-dG-Na<sub>inter</sub>. Two and four electrons were removed from Na<sub>ads</sub>-pG, Na<sub>ads</sub>-dG and Na<sub>ads</sub>-dG-Na<sub>inter</sub>, respectively. The Van der Waals (vdW) interactions were considered using a modified version of the vdW-DF, denoted as optB86b-vdW.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202421976-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202421976-bib-0032_R_d41648781e1680" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - J. Phys.: Condens. Matter Phys. Rev. B">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The projector augmented wave potentials were used with an energy cutoff of 600&nbsp;eV.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202421976-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202421976-bib-0033_R_d41648781e1686" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Phys. Rev. B">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The lattice constraints for graphene are 9.86   9.86 , with a vacuum layer larger than 20  perpendicular to the surface plane. A 4  4  1 Monkhorst-pack <i>k</i>-mesh scheme was used for geometry optimization, ensuring energy convergence of 1.0  10<sup>4</sup> meV&nbsp;atom<sup>1</sup> during the self-consistent field calculations. The convergence criteria for the atomic forces was set to 0.01&nbsp;eV&nbsp;<sup>1</sup>. The <i>k</i>-meshes were doubled for single-point calculation. The adsorption energies <i>E<sub>ad</sub></i> were calculated according to the equation:

                     <div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202421976-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                           <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/8b641789-2ae5-40c8-9125-cb9e56bdd716/adfm202421976-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202421976:adfm202421976-math-0001" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202421976-math-0001.png">
                              <semantics>
                                 <mrow>
                                    <msub>
                                       <mi>E</mi>
                                       <mrow>
                                          <mi>a</mi>
                                          <mi>d</mi>
                                       </mrow>
                                    </msub>
                                    <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                                    <msub>
                                       <mi>E</mi>
                                       <mrow>
                                          <mi>t</mi>
                                          <mi>o</mi>
                                          <mi>t</mi>
                                          <mi>a</mi>
                                          <mi>l</mi>
                                       </mrow>
                                    </msub>
                                    <mspace width="0.33em"></mspace>
                                    <mo linebreak="goodbreak"></mo>
                                    <msub>
                                       <mi>E</mi>
                                       <mrow>
                                          <mi>i</mi>
                                          <mi>o</mi>
                                          <mi>n</mi>
                                       </mrow>
                                    </msub>
                                    <mo linebreak="goodbreak"></mo>
                                    <msub>
                                       <mi>E</mi>
                                       <mrow>
                                          <mi>s</mi>
                                          <mi>l</mi>
                                          <mi>a</mi>
                                          <mi>b</mi>
                                       </mrow>
                                    </msub>
                                 </mrow>
                                 <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}{{E}_{ad}} = {{E}_{total}}\ - {{E}_{ion}} - {{E}_{slab}}\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                              </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>where <i>E<sub>total</sub></i>, <i>E<sub>ion</sub></i>, and <i>E<sub>slab</sub></i> are the energies of the adsorption system, adsorbed ion, and graphene slab, respectively. A more negative adsorption energy indicates a more stable adsorption configuration. Additionally, the charge density differences, and density of state (DOS) were extracted from the static calculation. The charge density difference was visualized by VESTA.</div>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
